<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15172961920307569889">We hear the world will end on a Saturday. Next Saturday, in fact. Just before dinner. Unfortunately, Sister Mary Loquacious of the Chattering Order has misplaced the Antichrist. The Four Horsemen of the Apocalypse ride motorcycles. And the representatives from Heaven and Hell have decided they actually <em>like</em> the human race...<br /><br />Have a nice Doomsday.</span>

              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_11865807').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_23190961">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23190961" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/969209-jeremy-zerbe" class="left imgcol" title="Jeremy Zerbe"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1422308831p2/969209.jpg" alt="Jeremy Zerbe" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/23190961?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 04, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/969209-jeremy-zerbe" class="user" title="Jeremy Zerbe">Jeremy Zerbe</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12067.Good_Omens" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060853983" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer23190961">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11243350026370404226">Remember back when funny books were funny? Back before you went to college and found out that Dave Barry and Carl Hiaasen weren't funny after all, but Samuel Beckett and Charles Dickens were hilarious? Remember when the words on the page didn't just make you smile wryly and shake your head in shame for humanity, but actually made you laugh out loud? Well, that's the kind of humor that Terry Pratchett and Neil Gaiman's 1990 release Good Omens brims with, and it is so damn good.<br /><br />The two British aut</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11243350026370404226">Remember back when funny books were funny? Back before you went to college and found out that Dave Barry and Carl Hiaasen weren't funny after all, but Samuel Beckett and Charles Dickens were hilarious? Remember when the words on the page didn't just make you smile wryly and shake your head in shame for humanity, but actually made you laugh out loud? Well, that's the kind of humor that Terry Pratchett and Neil Gaiman's 1990 release Good Omens brims with, and it is so damn good.<br /><br />The two British authors were both relatively unknown at the time of their partnership, having met when Gaiman (working as a journalist at the time) interviewed Pratchett on the success of his first major novel, The Colour of Magic. The two became quick friends and proceeded to write the 398 pages of the now cult classic novel by sending floppy disks through the mail and calling each other on the phone. Of course, that story is all explained in the appendix, provided you don't pick up an original printing of the book (if you do do that, however, you can probably sell it for quite a bit of change, so don't be discouraged by your lack of author interviews).<br /><br />But the real story at hand is, of course, the narrative of Good Omens itself--the tale of two friends, a demon named Crowley and an angel named Aziraphale who have spent all of human existence on earth and have rather come to like it, so when it comes time for the Apocalypse, they try to do whatever is in their powers to stop it. The cast of co-stars can only described as "vast," with some characters only popping in long enough for Aziraphale to take over their body or to go on a shooting rampage. The main other characters though, include: the Antichrist himself, a young boy named Adam, and his gang of friends; a witchfinder named Newton Pulsifer and his love interest, Anathema Device, who just happens to be a witch (and one whose ancestor, Agnes Nutter was burned at the stake by Newton's great-great-etc. grandfather, Thou-Shalt-Not-Commit-Adultery Pulsifer.<br /><br />Perhaps you're beginning to pick up on that sense of humor I mentioned?<br /><br />That's what makes this book so great. I'm sort of a sucker for religious humor (and religious horror movies), and I've read a lot of books about the End Days. This one has to rank near the top, maybe even as the downright finest. It's humor ranges from simple little comedic bits to social commentary on religion and the human race--but no matter how big or small the joke is, every one of them is attended to equally, and they are all funny because of that. Though some of the British jokes and references flew by me (a problem the authors usually account for in their humorous footnotes), I really did enjoy this book, all the way from the plot down to how it was written. It really is an impressive feat for a co-authored book to feel so seemless (this honestly almost puts shame to the excellent Stephen King/Peter Straub double-ups, The Talisman and Black House).<br /><br />So let down your guard, pack away that condescension that your professors poured into you Lit class after Lit class, and resist the urge to turn up your nose at any novel you can buy in trade paperback form in airports for $7.99 (but higher in Canada--oh wait, not anymore!). I actually laughed out loud as I read Good Omens. A few times, to be completely honest. And that's pretty impressive for a cynical, jaded old bastard like me.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11243350026370404226">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_96420927">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/96420927" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1713956-manny" class="left imgcol" title="Manny"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" alt="Manny" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/96420927?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 23, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1713956-manny" class="user" title="Manny">Manny</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12067.Good_Omens" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060853983" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=linguistics-and-philosophy" class="actionLinkLite">linguistics-and-philosophy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=well-i-think-its-funny" class="actionLinkLite">well-i-think-its-funny</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=too-sexy-for-maiden-aunts" class="actionLinkLite">too-sexy-for-maiden-aunts</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=parody-homage" class="actionLinkLite">parody-homage</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=story-review" class="actionLinkLite">story-review</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=pooh-dante" class="actionLinkLite">pooh-dante</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer96420927">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16533987006124482739">I somehow ended up reading them both simultaneously. So I couldn't help wondering<br /><br /><strong>What Madam Bovary Might Have Thought Of <em>Good Omens</em></strong><br /><br />Three days later, a package arrived; there was no return address, but she immediately recognised Rodolphe's hand. It contained a paperback novel, whose title was <em>Good Omens</em>. Feverishly, she cast herself over it. Her English was poor, but, with the aid of a dictionary, she persevered and soon made great progress.<br /><br />The more she read, the greater her bewilderment became.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16533987006124482739">I somehow ended up reading them both simultaneously. So I couldn't help wondering<br /><br /><strong>What Madam Bovary Might Have Thought Of <em>Good Omens</em></strong><br /><br />Three days later, a package arrived; there was no return address, but she immediately recognised Rodolphe's hand. It contained a paperback novel, whose title was <em>Good Omens</em>. Feverishly, she cast herself over it. Her English was poor, but, with the aid of a dictionary, she persevered and soon made great progress.<br /><br />The more she read, the greater her bewilderment became. The book at first reminded her of <em>Candide</em>, which she had surreptitiously read at the convent, but M. Voltaire's <em>ésprit</em> had been replaced by another ingredient she was unable to name; she suspected that it must be the strange English invention they called <em>humour</em>. All the personages were well-meaning and agreeable; the witches, the torturers of witches, the prostitutes, even the Demons of Hell; they were filled with kindness and compassion, and their worst faults amounted to an occasional mild irritability. Where were the indifference and thoughtless cruelty that surrounded her, and which had now become the very air she breathed? <br /><br />She did not know whether Rodolphe had sent her the book to comfort her or to mock her in her despair, and her futile attempts to resolve this question gradually resulted in an agonising headache. Her husband prescribed an infusion of valerian, and persuaded her to retire for the night; she lay sleepless in her bed a long time, until the drug finally took effect just as the sky was beginning to lighten. She dreamed of apocalyptic prophecies, red-headed women wielding swords, endless circles of horseless carriages, young boys with dogs.<br /><br />In the morning, she remembered that she should purchase some arsenic.<br />__________________________________<br /><br />It seemed unfair for this to be one-way. So, in the spirit of granting a right of reply, here's <br /><br /><strong>What Good Omens Might Have Thought Of Madam Bovary</strong><br /><br />"I saw this smashin' film yesterday on TV," said Adam, as the Them listened attentively. "It was called Madam Bovver-Boy -"<br /><br />"She was a lady skinhead?" interrupted Brian.<br /><br />"No, stupid," said Adam. "It's a French name. <em>Bovver-Boy</em>. By Flow-Bear."<br /><br />"You mean Madame Bovary, by Flaubert," said Wensleydale. "I read about it in The Encyclopaedia of World Literature."<br /><br />Adam gave him a withering glance. "That's what I said," he continued. "Madam Bovver-Boy, by Flow-Bear. She's married to this doctor, and he's dead borin', so she starts hangin' around with these two lovers, and then she maxes out her credit card, so she eats arsernick and poisons herself. The bit where she's dyin' of the arsernick is dead good. Her tongue's hanging out and she's screamin' -"<br /><br />"Why did she max out her credit card?" asked Pepper.<br /><br />"She was buying presents for her lovers," said Adam. "Roses an' boxes of chocolates an' stuff like that -"<br /><br />"I thought the lovers were supposed to give her presents?" said Brian dubiously. "My sister's boyfriend gave her this huge bunch of roses on Valentine's Day, and a box of Quality Street, and a balloon with -"<br /><br />"She gave them presents instead because it was a proto-feminist novel," explained Wensleydale authoritatively. "That's what The Encyclopaedia of World Literature says."<br /><br />Adam felt that his control of the situation was slipping, and decided to up the stakes. "It's all true," he said, in an exegetical move that would have had Flaubert scholars around the world clutching their foreheads. "<em>Based</em> on a true story," he added prudently, in case the The Encyclopaedia of World Literature happened to have opinions on the subject. <br /><br />Behind the bushes, Aziraphale raised an eyebrow. Crowley looked defensive. "Very loosely based," he whispered hastily. "I mean, I tempted her, it's my job you know, but Gustave changed the ending for dramatic purposes. Said it didn't work to have Rodolphe sort out her debts and then settle down in a cozy <em>ménage à quatre</em> with her, Léon and her husband. I told him that's what actually happened, but he insisted the arsenic worked better..."<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16533987006124482739">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_104377527">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/104377527" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="left imgcol" title="Nataliya"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1395089173p2/3672777.jpg" alt="Nataliya" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/104377527?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 03, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="user" title="Nataliya">Nataliya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12070.Good_Omens" title="Paperback 9780060853976" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=pratchett" class="actionLinkLite">pratchett</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=excellent-reads" class="actionLinkLite">excellent-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2012-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2012-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer104377527">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13731393204819196758"><br />In my personal hierarchy of books, this one comes a close second after Bulgakov's <em>The Master and Margarita</em>. What can I say - <strong>like (diabolical) father, like (infernal) son.</strong> <blockquote>"<em>It may help to understand human affairs to be clear that most of the great triumphs and tragedies of history are caused not by people being fundamentally good or fundamentally bad, but by people being fundamentally people.</em>"</blockquote><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335332i/694303._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />In a way, I can view this book as my own personal therapy session - that is, in addition to it being a <strong>h</strong></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13731393204819196758"><br />In my personal hierarchy of books, this one comes a close second after Bulgakov's <em>The Master and Margarita</em>. What can I say - <strong>like (diabolical) father, like (infernal) son.</strong> <blockquote>"<em>It may help to understand human affairs to be clear that most of the great triumphs and tragedies of history are caused not by people being fundamentally good or fundamentally bad, but by people being fundamentally people.</em>"</blockquote><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335332i/694303._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />In a way, I can view this book as my own personal therapy session¹ - that is, in addition to it being a <strong>hilarious dry-humor take on Armageddon and the ever-dual nature of humanity, its highs and lows and our ability to be both, frequently almost at the same time.</strong><blockquote>¹ As a kid, I had a habit of getting into the books clearly not meant for my age - like, for instance, <a title="The Omen by David Seltzer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/226162.The_Omen">The Omen</a>, featuring the world's most infamous tricycle. My eight-year-old self was petrified. For months, I had nightmares, was scared of dogs, mistrustful of tricycles and had an irrational dislike of the number "666". <br /><br />Eight-year-olds with overactive imaginations were really NOT the intended audience of *that* book, after all.</blockquote><u>WARNING: THERE WILL BE MILD SPOILERS. BECAUSE I CAN.</u><br /><br /><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335332i/694304._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />As predicted by the titular 17th century witch <strong>Agnes Nutter</strong> in her extremely <s>nice</s> accurate book of prophecies, handed down through centuries to her last living "professional descendant" Anathema Device, Armageddon is quickly approaching. <strong>The four Horsepersons of the Apocalypse have set out on their way, and that must mean things are getting serious.</strong><br /><blockquote>
  <em>- You're Hells Angels, then? What chapter are you from?"[...]<br />- REVELATIONS, CHAPTER SIX.<br />-----------<br />“Death and Famine and War and Pollution continued biking towards Tadfield. And Grievous Bodily Harm, Cruelty To Animals, Things Not Working Properly Even After You've Given Them A Good Thumping but secretly No Alcohol Lager, and Really Cool People travelled with them.” </em>
</blockquote> And this upcoming major event is a source of some serious worry for eternal-enemies-turned-reluctant-friends <strong>Aziraphale</strong><em> (An angel, and a part-time rare book dealer)</em> and <strong>Crowley</strong> <em>(An Angel who did not so much Fall as Saunter Vaguely Downwards)</em>, the duo who, after six thousand years, have "gone native" and would infinitely prefer this world to the future where either side wins - <strong>the future (oh the horror!) without good music or bookshops or sushi restaurants.</strong> And so the unlikely allies decide to band together to prevent the end of the world.<blockquote>"<em>That's how it goes, you think you're on top of the world, and suddenly they spring Armageddon on you.</em>"</blockquote> Except things do not go as planned.<br /><br /><img width="160" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335332i/694305._SY540_.jpg" />...<img width="210" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335332i/694306._SY540_.jpg" /><br />(The lovely images above can be seen <a rel="nofollow" href="http://lerms.deviantart.com/art/Good-Omens-74957787">here</a> and <a rel="nofollow" href="http://daidaishar.deviantart.com/art/Adam-Young-169383880">here</a>).<br /><br /><strong>You see, the young '<em>Adversary, Destroyer of Kings, Angel of the Bottomless Pit, Great Beast that is called Dragon, Prince of This World, Father of Lies, Spawn of Satan, and Lord of Darkness</em>', due to an unforeseen turn of circumstances, happens to grow up outside of the influence of either celestial/underworld force</strong>. <br /><br />Aptly named Adam, he grows up - as his name suggests - perfectly <strong>human</strong>, in a little corner of paradise - the English village of Lower Tadfield. But Agnes Nutter is never wrong, and as her prophesies suggest, shortly after Adam's eleventh birthday (on which he DOES get a much wanted <s>Hellhound</s> Dog) the events of the end of the world are set in their inevitable, <strong>ineffable</strong> motion. <br /><br /><strong>But Adam's essential humanity puts a new spin on this old story:</strong><blockquote>"<em>Something was happening inside his head.<br /><br />It was aching. Thoughts were arriving there without him having to think them. Something was saying, You can do something, Adam Young. You can make it all better. You can do anything you want. And what was saying this to him was ... him. Part of him, deep down. Part of him that had been attached to him all these years and not really noticed, like a shadow. It was saying: yes, it's a rotten world. It could have been great. But now it's rotten, and it's time to do something about it. That's what you're here for. To make it all better.</em>"</blockquote>The problem with <s>the Antichrist</s> Adam is that he, a human eleven-year-old boy, is fueled - unexpectedly - by Love and righteous indignation. <strong>He loves this world, and he, coming into his power, wants a very human thing - he wants to make it better.</strong> Don't we all? But do (and can?) his good intentions make any difference in the way things have been prophesied to go?<blockquote>
<em>"It's like you said the other day," said Adam. "You grow up readin' about pirates and cowboys and spacemen and stuff, and jus' when you think the world's all full of amazin' things, they tell you it's really all dead whales and chopped-down forests and nucular waste hangin' about for millions of years. 'Snot worth growin' up for, if you ask my opinion.</em>"</blockquote><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335332i/694307.jpg" /><br /><br />If there is one thing this book gets through it's the belief in humanity. Not in its wickedness or goodness or anything like that. <strong>Just humanity, in all the multifaceted nature of it, in its righteousness and wretchedness, love and cruelty, strengths and weaknesses, stupidity and wisdom.</strong> <blockquote>"<em>And just when you'd think they were more malignant than ever Hell could be, they could occasionally show more grace than Heaven ever dreamed of. Often the same individual was involved. It was this free-will thing, of course. It was a bugger.</em>"</blockquote><strong>Sometimes, maybe, when left to our own devices, when not preached to in one way or another, we can perhaps develop into flawed but hopefully decent beings</strong> - like Adam, named after the first human in the prophetic fashion, after all. Because what makes life interesting, as a particular angel and demon would loudly attest to, is precisely the combination of good and evil, nice and nasty, mean and kind that we all possess, in the precarious and miraculous balance that is the true treasure of humanity. Because it makes us act like people.<blockquote>"<em>I don't see what's so triflic about creating people as people and then gettin' upset 'cos they act like people,” said Adam severely. “Anyway, if you stopped tellin' people it's all sorted out after they're dead, they might try sorting it all out while they're alive.</em>"</blockquote>And maybe, just maybe, due to our always-balancing nature on the borderline between two conflicting universes that we, humans, inhabit, we will be able to eventually figure it out - without anyone messing with our heads, filling them with the Good or the Evil, endlessly preaching what they believe to be true - but simple letting us be ourselves. <strong>Maybe we will figure things out on our own.</strong><blockquote>"<em>Adam stood smiling at the two of them, a small figure perfectly poised exactly between Heaven and Hell.<br /><br />Crowley grabbed Aziraphale's arm. "You know what happened?" he hissed excitedly. "He was left alone! He grew up human! He's not Evil Incarnate or Good Incarnate, he's just ... a human incarnate.</em>"</blockquote>==============================<br />....<img width="300" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380335332i/694308.jpg" />....<br /><br /><strong>The brilliant Pratchett/Gaiman duo</strong> provides everything these two authors are famous for - easy readability, dry intelligent sarcasm-infused humor, seamless plot that pushes the limits of imagination while staying perfectly grounded in (albeit fantastical) reality, and first and foremost, very apt observations about human nature, as well as (in a true Pratchett-like way) a complete irreverence for the set-in-stone beliefs and ideas. At times it's easy for those familiar with their respective styles to tell which one of them penned which part, at times it's impossible - but it doesn't matter as their writing styles blend together so well, so seamlessly, so seemingly effortlessly.<br /><br />This is an excellent book - both funny and serious, at times utterly unpredictable, at times baffling, at times logical. It's a pleasure to read, and a pleasure to seriously reflect upon after having a good laugh. And for all of that it gets <strong>the ineffable five stars.</strong><blockquote>
  <em>He stared down at the golden curls of the Adversary, Destroyer of Kings, Angel of the Bottomless Pit, Great Beast that is called Dragon, Prince of This World, Father of Lies, Spawn of Satan, and Lord of Darkness.<br /><br />"You know," he concluded, after a while, "I think he actually looks like an Adam."</em>
</blockquote>--------<br /><strong>June 2013:</strong> I just listened to this one on audiobook - AGAIN. I love it more and more with each time I do. I have to advise - if you plan to listen to this one, please get <strong>the British version with Stephen Briggs as the narrator</strong> (Isis Publishing) - it is lightyears better than Harper Audio. Stephen Briggs is amazing!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13731393204819196758">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_14815427">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14815427" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/786347-derek" class="left imgcol" title="Derek"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Derek" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14815427?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 03, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/786347-derek" class="user" title="Derek">Derek</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14815427">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13630103575704773360">Overall, this book was a huge disappointment for me. I’d heard so many good things about it and had been meaning to read it for years. When I finally started it, I was about 20 pages into it and thought, “Yes! This is going to be one of the most entertaining books I’ve ever read.” It was like reading <em>The Hitchhiker’s Guide</em> for the first time again. It was witty and fast-paced and had so many brilliant things to say about society and religion. And then about halfway through, I realized that I jus</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13630103575704773360">Overall, this book was a huge disappointment for me. I’d heard so many good things about it and had been meaning to read it for years. When I finally started it, I was about 20 pages into it and thought, “Yes! This is going to be one of the most entertaining books I’ve ever read.” It was like reading <em>The Hitchhiker’s Guide</em> for the first time again. It was witty and fast-paced and had so many brilliant things to say about society and religion. And then about halfway through, I realized that I just wasn't really laughing as much anymore. <br /><br />The story seemed to get bogged down with characters that weren’t that interesting for me to read about and who I often found a bit annoying. Most notably, the 11-year-old Antichrist and his friends seemed really out of place in the book. They felt like they would have been at home in a Norman Rockwell painting (or British equivalent)—a ragtag bunch of kids with slingshots and backyard adventures. I think that I was supposed to find them and their inane conversations entertaining, maybe even a little bit cute, but every time they came around, which felt like quite a bit, I thought that the book just screeched to a halt.<br /><br />Most of the other characters in the book were great fun to read—the demon and the angel and their strange sort of friendship was easily the best part of the book for me. The four horsemen of the apocalypse were also a lot of fun, at least most of the time. Overall, the premise was brilliant. What a disappointment for me that it just couldn’t keep it together throughout. The ending seemed contrived and the resolution was just unsatisfying for me. I know that so many people loved this book. I wish that I could have enjoyed it as much as they did.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13630103575704773360">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_17641119">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17641119" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/986519-kyle-nakamura" class="left imgcol" title="Kyle Nakamura"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1205375277p2/986519.jpg" alt="Kyle Nakamura" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/17641119?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 12, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/986519-kyle-nakamura" class="user" title="Kyle Nakamura">Kyle Nakamura</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12067.Good_Omens" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060853983" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone who doesn't take their spirituality too seriously</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer17641119">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4278117377453711357"> This has got to be one of the funniest satires I've ever read. I suppose the closest comparison I could make is to describe it as a literary sibling to Dogma, but filtered through a distinctly British lense. That description doesn't really do the story justice, but that film definitely hits me in the same place as the book.<br /> The whole premise, and I'm not giving much away here, begins with the accidental "mis-placement" of the infant Antichrist during a complex baby-swapping procedure intended t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4278117377453711357"> This has got to be one of the funniest satires I've ever read. I suppose the closest comparison I could make is to describe it as a literary sibling to Dogma, but filtered through a distinctly British lense. That description doesn't really do the story justice, but that film definitely hits me in the same place as the book.<br /> The whole premise, and I'm not giving much away here, begins with the accidental "mis-placement" of the infant Antichrist during a complex baby-swapping procedure intended to kick off the Apocalypse. It's all down hill from there. <br /> This book is funny, irreverent, and at times surprisingly insightful. While some parts of the book may seem cliched or even kitschy, the book never puts up the pretense of being revolutionary or edgy. The characters, like any melodrama, are intended to be archetypal, so even the relatively predictable changes that occur seem appropriate in the context of the story. The surprises are really in the details, and in that regard the execution is brilliant. To give you an example, the devil Crowly drives a car in which the tape-deck will transform any tape placed in it into a copy of "Queen's Greatest Hits" within a very short period of time after one hits the play button. Now I love "Bohemian Rhapsody" as much as the next guy, but an eternity of nothing but THAT particular album?! That's the kind of devilry that works on many levels.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4278117377453711357">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1431586">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1431586" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="left imgcol" title="J.G. Keely"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" alt="J.G. Keely" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1431586?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 25, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="user" title="J.G. Keely">J.G. Keely</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=novel" class="actionLinkLite">novel</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=contemporary-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">contemporary-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=uk-and-ireland" class="actionLinkLite">uk-and-ireland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1431586">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9830501369502743755">I read this book before I tried to tackle Pratchett on his own merit, so I may have to retroactively skew this review based upon what I now know. The book is enjoyable, but may suffer from the fact that it represents its two authors at what seems to be their most basic states.<br /><br />There is no question as to the recognizability of both Gaiman's and Pratchett's respective styles here, but neither seems to add anything to the other. One of Gaiman's weaknesses is surely his general lack of humor. Anythin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9830501369502743755">I read this book before I tried to tackle Pratchett on his own merit, so I may have to retroactively skew this review based upon what I now know. The book is enjoyable, but may suffer from the fact that it represents its two authors at what seems to be their most basic states.<br /><br />There is no question as to the recognizability of both Gaiman's and Pratchett's respective styles here, but neither seems to add anything to the other. One of Gaiman's weaknesses is surely his general lack of humor. Anything that makes you laugh in his books isn't likely to qualify as a joke. While this could have been remedied by Pratchett's collaboration, his humor tends to be more groan-worthy than profound.<br /><br />It seemed to me that, by collaborating, both authors felt a need to simplify and de-personalize their respective styles, which for Gaiman meant an unfortunate loss of much of his dark charm, and for Pratchett that he was even more watered down than usual.<br /><br />I know a lot of people, especially fantasy fans, love this book, and I will admit that it is romp-y, easily digestible, and certainly doesn't betray the inclinations of either author. Unfortunately, it also doesn't surpass them or create anything new or interesting. The whole is less than the sum of its respective parts. However, certainly worth a read; if only to get a fix of Gaiman while waiting for him to actually finish his next book.<br /><br />UPDATE: After reading Gaiman's <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21930383">Anansi Boys</a>, I have come to find that he can be quite uproariously and side-splittingly funny. I am now unsure just what part Pratchett played in Good Omens at all.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://starsbeetlesandfools.blogspot.com/2012/06/suggested-readings-in-fantasy.html">My Fantasy Book Suggestions</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9830501369502743755">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class="brown_comment" id="comment_143008382">
  <div class="xhr_comment_photo">
    	<a href="/user/show/33734555-aaron-adamson" alt="Aaron Adamson"><img alt="33734555" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1407377217p1/33734555.jpg" /></a>
  </div>
  <div class="xhr_comment_body">
    <a href="/user/show/33734555-aaron-adamson" style="font-weight:bold">Aaron Adamson</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer15267968163807874927">Thanks for the reply. It seems to me that the tone is markedly different from Adams and Monty Python... in those cases, the plot tends to serve mostly</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15267968163807874927">Thanks for the reply. It seems to me that the tone is markedly different from Adams and Monty Python... in those cases, the plot tends to serve mostly as thin scaffolding for absurdist humor to play in, with little care for actual tension or character development. On the other hand, it seemed to me that the humor in Good Omens was more situational and the plot more developed by comparison.<br /><br />In any case, surely we shouldn't call works unoriginal merely for employing droll British absurdism? A creative concept is still every bit as inventive even if it is packaged in familiar tone, in my opinion. I would go as far as to say that works that innovate in too many directions at once struggle to find a readership. It can be a good decision, mechanically, for an author to focus narrative experimentation on one or two aspects of a novel's structure.<br /><br />Anyhow, please don't interpret my comments as negative, I think it's a genuinely interesting discussion. What standards should authors be held to for originality of voice and tone? And, should the variability of taste in comedic works be taken into account in our criticism of them? As was mentioned earlier by another poster, few things are as subjective as comedy.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15267968163807874927">...more</a>

<div class="brown_comment" id="comment_142384492">
  <div class="xhr_comment_photo">
    	<a href="/user/show/31997285-jillian" alt="Jillian"><img alt="31997285" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1422681363p1/31997285.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="xhr_comment_body">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer374366936">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2181923847896699940">No getting around it, it IS funny! Clever satire that’s harmlessly irreverent. I wasn’t rolling on the floor or anything but I had 4 (I counted) laugh-out-loud moments, a few good giggles &amp; a smile on my face throughout. A great story that moves along very nicely, as Good and Evil (as represented by the angel Aziraphale &amp; the demon Crowley) join forces to try &amp; avert the apocalypse. Definitely held my interest. <br />The interplay between these two was what really made the story, liked it</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2181923847896699940">No getting around it, it IS funny! Clever satire that’s harmlessly irreverent. I wasn’t rolling on the floor or anything but I had 4 (I counted) laugh-out-loud moments, a few good giggles &amp; a smile on my face throughout. A great story that moves along very nicely, as Good and Evil (as represented by the angel Aziraphale &amp; the demon Crowley) join forces to try &amp; avert the apocalypse. Definitely held my interest. <br />The interplay between these two was what really made the story, liked it when they put their differences aside &amp; just hung out. Another highlight was the 4 horsemen of the Apocalypse; Death, War, Famine &amp; Pollution (yes pollution...I know, it’s supposed to be Pestilence - read the book:)) who make their appearance as Hell’s Angels. Actually the book is loaded with great characters, there's even a cute little dog, The Hound from Hell morphed into a cat chasing mongrel. <br /><br />Literature defined. <em>Simple enough to grasp but complex enough to inspire thought.</em> <br />Yeh, it’s that. You might find yourself contemplating some pretty deep stuff, like <strong>"the big ineffable plan" </strong> or the murky line defining mankind as basicly good or evil. Or maybe not, I’ve been over-thinking everything lately.<br /><br /><em>"Crowley found devil worshippers a little embarrassing. You couldn’t actually be rude to them, but you couldn’t help feeling about them the same way that, say, a Vietnam vet would feel about someone who wears combat gear to Neighborhood Watch meetings."</em><br /><br /><strong>As Fantasy / Humor – 5 well deserved stars </strong></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17079462596524373366">I find if very difficult not to like a book about how plans for Armageddon hit a snag when a scatterbrained Satanic nun misplaces the Antichrist. <br /><br />Many of the reviews of Good Omens compare it to Douglas Adams. There are some similarities in that much of the story occurs outside the bounds of normal reality, it's genuinely funny, and very British. But I found the overall tone to be softer, less snarky, and more intentionally philosophical in nature. <br /><br />While the book is very entertaining, it also ask</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17079462596524373366">I find if very difficult not to like a book about how plans for Armageddon hit a snag when a scatterbrained Satanic nun misplaces the Antichrist. <br /><br />Many of the reviews of Good Omens compare it to Douglas Adams. There are some similarities in that much of the story occurs outside the bounds of normal reality, it's genuinely funny, and very British. But I found the overall tone to be softer, less snarky, and more intentionally philosophical in nature. <br /><br />While the book is very entertaining, it also asks some exceedingly interesting questions. In particular: does the Antichrist have free will?<br /></span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer4410989">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10962405686284307373">I actually feel a little bad giving this 2 stars, since I see so many reviews of people who loved this book. Unfortunately, I'm just not one of them. <br /><br />I usually like Pratchett's work, and there are a few comic touches that I liked here, but overall the unbelievably slow pace of the latter half of this story nearly drove me bonkers. It skips over about 10 years in a few chapters, and then camps out at 6 hours 'til doomsday for hundreds of pages. The dialogue of the children was tiresome, and the o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10962405686284307373">I actually feel a little bad giving this 2 stars, since I see so many reviews of people who loved this book. Unfortunately, I'm just not one of them. <br /><br />I usually like Pratchett's work, and there are a few comic touches that I liked here, but overall the unbelievably slow pace of the latter half of this story nearly drove me bonkers. It skips over about 10 years in a few chapters, and then camps out at 6 hours 'til doomsday for hundreds of pages. The dialogue of the children was tiresome, and the only character remotely 3-dimensional was the demon Crowley. I began skimming pages before giving up altogether on it a few chapters from the end. I would say it's not my style of book, but that's not true: again, I like Pratchett's other works, and I'm a Douglas Adams fan as well. This one just didn't hit my funny bone, I guess. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_42680391">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42680391" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1720620-anne" class="left imgcol" title="Anne"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1415374035p2/1720620.jpg" alt="Anne" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/42680391?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 23, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1720620-anne" class="user" title="Anne">Anne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12067.Good_Omens" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060853983" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">jill joyce</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=paranormal" class="actionLinkLite">paranormal</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=read-in-2009" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2009</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=neil-gaiman" class="actionLinkLite">neil-gaiman</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=wishlist" class="actionLinkLite">wishlist</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=kindle" class="actionLinkLite">kindle</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=read-in-2014" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2014</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer42680391">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15394201986320298889"><strong>Reread 2014</strong><br />I read American Gods not too long ago, and while I liked it, it didn't turn out to be a favorite. So I wondered if maybe I shouldn't go back and check this one out. You know, see if it was really as good as I remembered?<br />Huh.<br />It was actually <em>better</em>. Hilarious! <br />The 5 star rating stands!<br /><br /><strong>Original review 2009</strong><br /><br />Good Omens is going to have to go down as one of my favorites! I wouldn't say that I laughed out loud, but I snorted once or twice and smiled the whole way through! Who would have thoug</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15394201986320298889"><strong>Reread 2014</strong><br />I read American Gods not too long ago, and while I liked it, it didn't turn out to be a favorite. So I wondered if maybe I shouldn't go back and check this one out. You know, see if it was really as good as I remembered?<br />Huh.<br />It was actually <em>better</em>. Hilarious! <br />The 5 star rating stands!<br /><br /><strong>Original review 2009</strong><br /><br />Good Omens is going to have to go down as one of my favorites! I wouldn't say that I laughed out loud, but I snorted once or twice and smiled the whole way through! Who would have thought the apocalypse could be so funny?! Evidently Neil Gaiman and Terry Pratchett. Personally, I thought it was a great "coming of age" story about a boy named Adam Young. <br /><br />Warning: If you are one of those people who wear a "What Would Jesus Do?" bracelet and believe that the purple teletubby really is gay, this is not the book for you. Just put it down and grab something by C.S. Lewis.<br /><br />The story centers around an angel and a demon who have spent thousands of years on earth together, and have quite a good working relationship. Everything is running along smoothly until Crowley (the demon) is charged with delivering the antichrist to his new family. In other words, handing over the Spawn of Hell to a bunch of (satanic) nuns who will switch him out with a human baby who has just been born. Once the deed is done, Crowley decides to enlist Aziraphale (the angel) to help him stop the coming apocalypse. They have eleven years before the boy reaches his "full potential"... </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15394201986320298889">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_4529525">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4529525" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/96664-samantha" class="left imgcol" title="Samantha"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1436848311p2/96664.jpg" alt="Samantha" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/4529525?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 07, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/96664-samantha" class="user" title="Samantha">Samantha</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12067.Good_Omens" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060853983" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone except those with no sense of humor or those who take the Book of Revelations seriously</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/96664-samantha?shelf=funny-satirical" class="actionLinkLite">funny-satirical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer4529525">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4959425064240845692">Oh. My. God.<br /><br />This was one of the funniest books I have ever read. The writing was phenomenal and I could see myself and others I know in many of the quirky characters.<br /><br />Good and Evil's earthly representatives discover that the time for the Apocalyse has arrived and they're not too happy about it. You see, they've grown to like life on Earth. And besides, Evil (with a capital 'E') itself couldn't possibly do worse things to mankind than what mankind does to itself.<br /><br />And the antichrist's name is Adam,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4959425064240845692">Oh. My. God.<br /><br />This was one of the funniest books I have ever read. The writing was phenomenal and I could see myself and others I know in many of the quirky characters.<br /><br />Good and Evil's earthly representatives discover that the time for the Apocalyse has arrived and they're not too happy about it. You see, they've grown to like life on Earth. And besides, Evil (with a capital 'E') itself couldn't possibly do worse things to mankind than what mankind does to itself.<br /><br />And the antichrist's name is Adam, for cryin' out loud! (Not to mention that his trusty sidekick, aptly named "Dog", rather likes being a <em>real dog</em> and doesn't want to go back to being a hellhound; he'd miss the smells!)<br /><br />My favorite line (and it was a tough decision, believe me!):<br /><br /><em>"This is how Newton Pulsifer looked as a man: if he went into a phone booth and changed, he might manage to come out looking like Clark Kent." </em><br /><br />And don't forget the <em>other</em> Four Horsemen of the Apocalypse - especially Treading in Dogshit (formerly All Foreigners Especially the French, formerly Things Not Working Properly Even When You've Given Them a Good Thumping, never actually No Alcohol Lager, briefly Embarrassing Personal Problems, formerly known as Skuzz). By the way, he ended up, after an unfortunate accident, calling himself People Covered in Fish.<br /><br />I was a little disappointed with the ending, though. It seemed a little too neat and easy. So that's why it didn't get that fifth star from me.<br /><br />Great book! The friend who suggested <em>Clan of the Cave Bear</em> to me (you know who you are!) has totally redeemed herself with this one.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1260778084">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1260778084" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/16628840-mario" class="left imgcol" title="Mario"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450462116p2/16628840.jpg" alt="Mario" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1260778084?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 28, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/16628840-mario" class="user" title="Mario">Mario</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12067.Good_Omens" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060853983" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/16628840-mario?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/16628840-mario?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/16628840-mario?shelf=best-of-the-best" class="actionLinkLite">best-of-the-best</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/16628840-mario?shelf=own-read" class="actionLinkLite">own-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1260778084">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11023010554407901880"><em>“I don't see what's so triffic about creating people as people and then gettin' upset cos' they act like people", said Adam severely. "Anyway, if you stopped tellin' people it's all sorted out after they're dead, they might try sorting it all out while they're alive.” </em><br /><br /><br />Neil Gaiman + Terry Pratchett = Perfection<br /><br />Let me start this review with saying that if you're not so open minded hardcore religious person. do yourself a favor and don't read this book 'cause you might end up really, <em>really</em> hating</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11023010554407901880"><em>“I don't see what's so triffic about creating people as people and then gettin' upset cos' they act like people", said Adam severely. "Anyway, if you stopped tellin' people it's all sorted out after they're dead, they might try sorting it all out while they're alive.” </em><br /><br /><br />Neil Gaiman + Terry Pratchett = Perfection<br /><br />Let me start this review with saying that if you're not so open minded hardcore religious person. do yourself a favor and don't read this book 'cause you might end up really, <em>really</em> hating it.<br /><br />Now off to the real review.<br /><br />I can positively say that (at least in my opinion) this is one of the best books ever written (okay, maybe I'm being a little subjective).<br /><br />I feel like when reading Gaiman's books, there is always a positive and a negative thing (at least in my case). The positive is: The book you are reading might just be one of the best book you've ever read. And a negative is: That book has to finish, and when it does, you don't have a clue what to do with your life. And I feel that the humor Pratchett brought to the story, just made it that much harder to finish the book and say goodbye to this brilliant story.<br /><br />Like I already said, this book was brilliant. I can easily say that a book never made me laugh as hard as this one did (I physically laughed my ass off multiple times while reading). But apart from that, this book was also very dark (and I didn't realize that until the very end). There are quotes in this book that just make you stop reading and think for a second. I feel like that through humor, they made some <em>really</em> good points about religion.<br /><br />And also, this book had one of the best characters ever created. Crowley: A demon who always wear glasses, drives a Bentley and listens to Queen. I mean, whats not to love?! I also loved chemistry between Crowley and Aziraphale. Actually, Aziraphale was my second favorite character.<br /><br />This was my first Pratchett's book (now I feel ashamed to say that), but it certainly won't be my last. I can already say that Pratchett was as big of a genius as Gaiman is. <br /><br />I don't think that there's anything else to say, except that if you haven't read this book but you like either Gaiman or Pratchett, do yourself a favor and go read it. I can guarantee that you won't regret it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11023010554407901880">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1132472527">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1132472527" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="left imgcol" title="Lyn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428721461p2/5253785.jpg" alt="Lyn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1132472527?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 25, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="user" title="Lyn">Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12067.Good_Omens" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060853983" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1132472527">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6404890647716831809">The year is 2114 and in an upstairs apartment in Lower Tadfield, Oxfordshire, England four people – Marge, Ron, Neville and Madam Tracey – sit around a table. They are gathered for a séance.<br /><br />Madam Tracey: I can feel my spirit guide approaching.<br /><br />Marge: Ooooo, this is exciting!<br /><br />Madam Tracey: [In a dark brown voice] How! [Then in her normal voice] Geronimo is that you? [And again in the deep voice] Yes, this’n is me.<br /><br />Neville: This is just like in that old book by Terry Pratchett and Neil Gaiman, Good O</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6404890647716831809">The year is 2114 and in an upstairs apartment in Lower Tadfield, Oxfordshire, England four people – Marge, Ron, Neville and Madam Tracey – sit around a table. They are gathered for a séance.<br /><br />Madam Tracey: I can feel my spirit guide approaching.<br /><br />Marge: Ooooo, this is exciting!<br /><br />Madam Tracey: [In a dark brown voice] How! [Then in her normal voice] Geronimo is that you? [And again in the deep voice] Yes, this’n is me.<br /><br />Neville: This is just like in that old book by Terry Pratchett and Neil Gaiman, Good Omens, I mean JUST like it!<br /><br />Madam Tracey: [In the Geronimo voice] How you mean?<br /><br />Ron: I read that book, you’re right; there is a spiritual medium who pretends to be led into the spirit world by Geronimo!<br /><br />Madam Tracey: Eh, gentlemen, please, you must concentrate … Um, hey, wait a minute; I really am feeling a presence!<br /><br />Freddy Mercury: [singing] Can anybody find me, somebody to love?<br /><br />Neville: What?? Who’s that? Where did that voice come from, is that Freddy Mercury?<br /><br />Freddy: Yes, Neville, it is I, Freddy Mercury, coming to you from the Great Beyond! This séance has created a paranormal gateway by the mention of Pratchett and Gaiman’s collaboration.<br /><br />Terry Pratchett: Hello! What have we here?<br /><br />Neil Gaiman: It appears that we have intruded upon a séance, I feel invited, don’t you?<br /><br />Terry: I do, as well, Neil, and how is everyone tonight?<br /><br />[the table sits in stunned, awkward silence]<br /><br />Freddy: Onstage, I was a devil. But I was hardly a social reject, may we be included in your séance, you were attempting to contact the spirit world weren’t you?<br /><br />Marge: Um, well, I wanted to speak to my deceased sister Pamela.<br /><br />Terry: Now there’s a lovely girl, dear, dear Pamela.<br /><br />Neville: Wait! I see, we mentioned your book Good Omens and we are here with Madam Tracey, and …<br /><br />Neil: And here we are, lovely to join you, also nice to see Lower Tadfield still standing after all this time, and that you remember us. Yes, Good Omens was a first-rate occult book, full of catchy prophecies and witty Pratchett sayings.<br /><br />Terry: Yes, indeed, like this one: “God does not play dice with the universe; He plays an ineffable game of His own devising, which might be compared, from the perspective of any of the other players [i.e. everybody], to being involved in an obscure and complex variant of poker in a pitch-dark room, with blank cards, for infinite stakes, with a Dealer who won't tell you the rules, and who smiles all the time.”<br /><br />Neil: Yes, that was a good one, and pivotal for that story. Also, here was one that I very much liked: “Most books on witchcraft will tell you that witches work naked. This is because most books on witchcraft were written by men.” Isn’t that right, Madam Tracey?<br /><br />Madam Tracey: Um, er, ah …<br /><br />Ron: What was so special about Good Omens that could create a spiritual gateway to open up and visit us here?<br /><br />Terry: Good Omens was about the end of the world, about a spiritual conflict, but mainly about being human, and how even angels and demons, yes even the Anti-Christ can find happiness here on earth and so we should too, or some such balderdash, I recall it selling rather well.<br /><br />Neville: The end of the world? Is it coming?<br /><br />Freddy: Sooner than you would think, Neville, but then I’m just a musical prostitute, my dear.<br /><br />Neil: Armageddon is scheduled to begin next week.<br /><br />[The table sits in horrified silence]<br /><br />Neil: Naaah! Just messing with you! [All the ghosts laugh]<br /><br />H.P. Lovecraft: Hi guys, interrupting a séance? Can I join in? Good Omens was great, by the way.<br /><br />Terry: Well, thank you, Yank, that mean’s a lot coming from an old spook like you …<br />Madam Tracey: OK, EVERYBODY OUT!! This is MY séance<br /><br />Neil: Really?? How rude, Madam Tracey. Fine, we’re leaving; I hear there’s a Wiccan coven meeting over in Berkshire. By the way, “Madam” Tracey – who’s real name is Marjory Potts, and who is fooling you three here for money – I’ll be letting the real Geronimo know about your needing a spiritual guide, I’m sure he would love to oblige, ta ta!<br /><br /><img width="400" height="250" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448497821i/17150460.jpg" /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_19995573">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19995573" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/752259-ken" class="left imgcol" title="Ken"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1397157389p2/752259.jpg" alt="Ken" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/19995573?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 04, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/752259-ken" class="user" title="Ken">Ken</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12070.Good_Omens" title="Paperback 9780060853976" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/752259-ken?shelf=devil" class="actionLinkLite">devil</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/752259-ken?shelf=satan" class="actionLinkLite">satan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer19995573">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14314979911010761146">It is really hard to write good literary satire. Simple fact is that often satire goes too far over to the side of parody. When it crosses that line, it becomes bad mimicry rather than true satire. Think what This Is Spinal Tap would have been like if Michael McKean, Christopher Guest, and Harry Shearer just did an impression of the guys from Saxon – it would be funny for five minutes (if you actually knew who Saxon was) but ultimately the joke would get old. Over-parody leads to a stale joke an</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14314979911010761146">It is really hard to write good literary satire. Simple fact is that often satire goes too far over to the side of parody. When it crosses that line, it becomes bad mimicry rather than true satire. Think what This Is Spinal Tap would have been like if Michael McKean, Christopher Guest, and Harry Shearer just did an impression of the guys from Saxon – it would be funny for five minutes (if you actually knew who Saxon was) but ultimately the joke would get old. Over-parody leads to a stale joke and then you have an author who is just winking at his readers. After all, is Rich Little really that funny?<br /><br /><a target="_blank" href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12070.Good_Omens_The_Nice_and_Accurate_Prophecies_of_Agnes_Nutter_Witch">
  <em>Good Omens: The Nice and Accurate Prophecies of Agnes Nutter, Witch</em>
</a> could’ve descended into a really bad parody, especially considering that co-authors Neil Gaiman and Terry Pratchett originally intended it as a send-up of Richard Crompton’s <em>William</em> books (ask your friends from the UK). The initial title they had conceived was <em>William the Antichrist</em>. But Gaiman and Pratchett took the joke farther out -- much farther out -- satirizing everything from the Bible to <em>The Omen</em> to modern English society. The cast of characters includes a sect of extremely loquacious nuns secretly in the employ of hell (The Chattering Order of Saint Beryl), Pollution as the replacement for a now retired Pestilence (thanks to the invention of Penicillin), a bibliophile Angel (known as Aziraphale) who is not so sure he wants heaven to win, a Demon who is more concerned with his antique Bentley than stealing souls, the slacker descendents of Matthew Hopkins, Witchfinder, and even Agnes Nutter who lives up to her name. This makes for a concoction that is rife with sharp, pinpointed jokes that still hold up and still retain their bite.<br /><br />Simply put, it is amazing satire. It the equivalent to reading a Monty Python film and comes as close to matching the sheer genius <em>The Life of Brian</em> as one could get in a novel. In an opening sequence, we’re introduced to Crowley, a demon who has come to enjoy his life on earth and is not particularly enthralled with the idea of Armageddon. The only thing that irks him more is having to show up for the daily counting of the deeds with two other demons at a dreary cemetery at midnight. Never mind the traffic getting out of London, the real frustration for Crowley arises when he cannot explain to his fellow hellspawn that blocking all portable phone systems in central London will do more good for Satan than tempting a politician or a priest.<br /><br />“But you couldn’t tell that to demons like Hastur and Ligur. Fourteenth-century minds, the lot of them. Spending years picking away at one soul. Admittedly it was <em>craftsmanship</em>, but you had to think differently these days. Not big, but wide. With five billion people in the world you couldn’t pick the buggers off one by one any more; you had to spread your effort. But demons like Ligur and Hastur wouldn’t understand. They’d never thought up Welsh-language television for example. Or value-added tax. Or Manchester.”<br /><br />Manchester is of course Crowley’s proudest achievement as a demon. Or there is the slight episode where the mighty Kraken rises from the sea once more, directly under a whaling ship. <br /><br />	“There is a tiny metal thing above it. The kraken stirs. And ten billion sushi dinners cry out for vengeance.”<br /><br />Chapters such as that keep <em>Good Omens</em> chugging along at great pace. What is most obvious is that Pratchett and Gaiman had an absolute hoot writing the book. The interplay is fantastic, a grand piling on of ideas, where ultimately it doesn’t matter who originally conceived of which bits (much like the Pythons). <br /><br />If you could level any criticism at the book it is that the ending is so bloody nice. The writers literally pull the final punch and leave the reader with a very saccharine outcome after pages and pages of skewering most of modern society (from the 17th century onward). You come to this very perfectly resolved, somewhat hopeful ending, feeling as the writers feared appearing a little too cynical. Picture The Empire Strikes Back if Luke just suddenly strikes down Darth Vader rather than losing his hand (and discovering the true identity of his father).<br /><br />This is mostly due to the original concept of <em>William the Antichrist</em> -- or rather the character of Adam. While the character is an interesting parody of Damian from <em>The Omen</em>, he tends to drag the action down, giving the book a YA bent that it doesn’t need. After all, the cast of memorable characters is overloaded as it is and the book is simply much funnier when Adam is not around to slow up the pace. One could argue this was a necessary device, a way to cut the more biting parts of the book in order to have some contrast. But in the end, you can’t help but feel that the character could’ve been reduced to a minor one with the emphasis kept on Crowley and Aziraphale’s attempts to thwart their respective sides during the ensuing Armageddon.<br /><br />It is however somewhat of a nitpick because that flaw is greatly diminished by the overall wit and surgical skewering of all things the Apocalypse in <em>Good Omens</em>. About the only thing funnier is The Left Behind series, but those books are not intentionally humorous.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14314979911010761146">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/6239708?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 15, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/118723-e-h" class="user" title="E.H.">E.H.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone who likes to be happy</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/118723-e-h?shelf=theboxmarkeddone" class="actionLinkLite">theboxmarkeddone</a>
          </div>
